    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c2eb7909bea24321891ab2bb1792b657">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c7cc40e6-4290-444e-a0d2-810e298b4ba1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d47241371" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Large reserves, high capacity, and low cost are the core competitiveness of disordered carbon materials as excellent anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). And the existence and improper treatment of a large number of organic solid wastes will aggravate the burden on the environment, therefore, it is significant to transform wastes into carbon-based materials for sustainable energy utilization. Herein, a kind of hard carbon materials are reported with waste biomass-foam as the precursor, which can improve the sodium storage performance through pre-oxidation strategy. The introduction of oxygen-containing groups can promote structural cross-linking, and inhibit the melting and rearrangement of carbon structure during high-temperature carbonization that produces a disordered structure with a suitable degree of graphitization. Moreover, the micropore structure are also regulated during the high-temperature carbonization process, which is conducive to the storage of sodium ions in the low-voltage plateau region. The optimized sample as an electrode material exhibits excellent reversible specific capacity (308.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE, 90.1%). In addition, a full cell with the waste foam-derived hard carbon anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode is constructed with high ICE and energy density. This work provides an effective strategy to conversion the waste to high-value hard carbon anode for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307132-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307132-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In order to alleviate global warming and the energy crisis, the research and development of large-scale energy storage equipment have become the mainstream to achieve the storage of renewable energy sources,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0001_R_d47241363e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - eScience">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> among which lithium-ion batteries(LIBs) have attracted much attention due to its high energy density, long cycle life, clean and pollution-free characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0002_R_d47241363e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0003_R_d47241363e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the limited lithium (Li) reserves have restricted their further multi-use development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0004_R_d47241363e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Lett.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0005_R_d47241363e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Carbon Neutralization">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unlike Li, sodium (Na) reserves are abundant and have similar physical and chemical properties to Li, making sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) become the most promising supplementary devices for LIBs in the electrochemical energy storage field.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0006_R_d47241363e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Communi.">6</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0007_R_d47241363e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large-scale commercialization of SIBs is still limited by anode materials because the larger radius (1.02 Å) of Na will directly affect the phase stability and diffusion kinetics in the electrochemical reaction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0008_R_d47241363e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0009_R_d47241363e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, it is urgent to find a suitable anode material for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, hard carbon (HC) materials have become one of the most feasible candidate materials due to their abundant precursor resources, unique structure, and good conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0010_R_d47241363e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0011_R_d47241363e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) making the large-scale application of HC still remains a challenge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0012_R_d47241363e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0013_R_d47241363e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0014_R_d47241363e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Electrochim. Acta">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared graded porous carbon using peanut skin with a high reversible capacity of 431mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, however, the high-surface-area of the carbon anode leads to the low ICE (≈34%). To further promote the development of SIBs, it is necessary to develop an HC anode with high ICE to increase the energy density of the full cell. It is well known, the degree of graphitization has great relevance with ICE while the most common method was tuning the carbonization temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0015_R_d47241363e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0016_R_d47241363e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbon N Y">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The value of ICE appears the trend of first rising and then falling with the rising of temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0017_R_d47241363e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0018_R_d47241363e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the structure adjusted by this simple treatment has limitations to achieve great sodium storage performance of the HC anode. Therefore, a series of effective strategies such as pre-treatment techniques, hierarchical pore architectures, doping heteroatoms, and compounding with other materials have been widely applied.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0019_R_d47241363e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Mater. Adv.">19</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0020_R_d47241363e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the regulation of oxygen-containing groups seems like a novel strategy to construct HC materials because it not only infliuence the formation process of HC, but also plays a key role in the charge–discharge cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0021_R_d47241363e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Tang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0022_R_d47241363e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Res.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared disordered carbon anodes for SIBs by regulating the content of oxygen-containing functional groups in anthraquinone derivatives. Thanks to appropriate d-spacing and heteroatom content, the optimal sample ICE can reach 83%, demonstrating the importance of oxygen-containing functional groups for the microstructure and storage performance of carbon anodes. In addition, low price is also one of the biggest advantages of SIBs. Different from the polymer or other high-value carbon sources, using waste as precursors like waste tea,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0023_R_d47241363e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Ionics">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moroccan argan shells,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0024_R_d47241363e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> jute silk waste,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0025_R_d47241363e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> lotus stem<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0026_R_d47241363e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and navel orange peel,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0027_R_d47241363e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Diam Relat Mater">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can not only prepare high-capacity carbon with the suitable structure, but also improve the cost performance by converting useless waste into high value-added materials. Thus, a method that combines high performance, low cost and sustainability is still needed to improve the performance of waste-derived carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0028_R_d47241363e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon N Y">28</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0029_R_d47241363e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Mater. Technol.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, a unique waste foam-derived carbon (WFOC) was gained via pre-oxidation technology. The introduction of oxygenated functional groups is the primary cause to regulate graphitization degree. Besides to increasing the surface active site and promoting organic crosslinking between molecules, it can also prevent the structural rearrangement of carbon microcrystals during the carbonization process, hence reducing the degree of graphitization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0030_R_d47241363e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Mater. Chem. Front.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the micropore structure was also regulated during the high-temperature carbonization process, which enhances the capacitance contribution in the low-voltage area. The optimal WFOC250-1400 electrode maintains a greater reversible capacity (308.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) with an ICE of 90.1%. Moreover,&nbsp;a full cell with the waste foam-derived hard carbon anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode was constructed with high ICE and energy density. It is important to note that this research offers an efficient technique for SIBs to create effective waste-derived carbon materials and illustrates how to realize the conversion and exploitation of waste while maintaining ecological balance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307132-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307132-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The low-cost and environmentally friendly biomass foam is mainly composed of biomass starch,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0031_R_d47241363e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbohydr. Polym.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is generally used in shock prevention and transportation industries, including aircraft interior panels and crash pads.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0032_R_d47241363e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Starch/Staerke">32</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0033_R_d47241363e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Environ. Chem. Lett.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the majority of them are just burned or thrown away after usage, taking up space in public and harming the environment. It has greater amounts of carbon than other materials, which makes it a suitable precursor for high-performance hard carbon composites.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307132-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) show the typical synthesis of waste foam into hard carbon. First, abundant oxygen-containing groups were introduced as cross-linking active sites through an air oxidation process to obtain stable carbon precursors. Then, through the high-temperature carbonization process, hard carbon with a high degree of disorder and moderate interlayer spacing was obtained. And the thermal decomposition behavior of untreated biomass-based waste foam materials was investigated through Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), mass loss can be roughly divided into three main areas: 1) The desorption process of inherent moisture was seen &lt; 120&nbsp;°C, which corresponds to a mass loss of ≈ 5&nbsp;wt%; 2) There is a significant volume weight loss (60–80&nbsp;wt%) between 220 and 320&nbsp;°C, attributed to the carbonization process of the material; 3) At temperatures &gt; 400&nbsp;°C, volume loss only accounts for 10–12&nbsp;wt%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0034_R_d47241363e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J Energy Chem">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon may be attributed to the decomposition of biomass starch, the introduction of some oxygen-containing defects, and structural rearrangement in carbon-based materials. According to the analysis above, we pre-treat biomass-based waste foam with various temperatures that approach the starting and ending points of rapid mass loss temperature zone at air atmosphere with 2&nbsp;h, and the end-product waste-derived carbon with high-temperature carbonization at N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere is expressed as WFOC-T. Among them, the carbon yield of the WFC-T sample obtained by direct carbonization is 12.8%. And the total carbon yield of the WFOC-T sample obtained under low-temperature pre-oxidation treatment continues to increase up to 18.3% with increasing temperature (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307132-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5b35556e-c140-4bac-8ad4-9ab169435e8d/smll202307132-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5b35556e-c140-4bac-8ad4-9ab169435e8d/smll202307132-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cf379965-fbbe-45ce-9de5-f2f2cc296322/smll202307132-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5b35556e-c140-4bac-8ad4-9ab169435e8d/smll202307132-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307132-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of the synthesis of waste-derived carbon structures, b–d) HRTEM images and corresponding FFT patterns of WFC-1400, WFOC250-1400, and WFOC280-1400, respectively. The illustration at the upper right corner shows the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns of corresponding samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To well understand the structure variation of WFC-1400 and WFOC-T from the influence of oxygen defect, comparative results of three samples obtained by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) are demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The observation is that waste foam-derived hard carbon material synthesized by multiple carbonization has an irregular block structure after crushing, with a size distribution range of 2–15&nbsp;µm. To be more specific, WFC1400 exhibits a layer structure made of graphitized microcrystals with some small particles on the surface whereas the introduction of oxygen-containing groups increases the block structure in WFOC-T materials and gradually smoothes the surface. After excessive oxidation, the surface of WFOC-T material exhibits more fragmented particles. Then, the comparison results of high-resolution Transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images and selected area Electron diffraction (SAED) were used to further explore the microstructure of the three carbon-based materials, which is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0001">1b–d</a>. In these three samples, a clear short-range ordered vortex layer graphite microcrystalline structure were observed with no visible long-range highly graphitized structure, which means that the prepared samples are typical hard carbon, it's worth noting that during the pre-heating process in air, a large number of bent graphene microcrystals<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0035_R_d47241363e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0036_R_d47241363e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and closed nanopores<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0033_R_d47241363e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Environ. Chem. Lett.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> appear inside WFOC250-1400 and WFOC280-1400, which is conducive to generate more sodium storage sites. As a contrast, only some thick stacks and smaller pore was generated in WFC-1400 which without additional preheating in the air atmosphere. All the samples were proven to be disordered hard carbon materials by the emergence of weak dispersed diffraction rings in the SAED patterns. Moreover, the increase of interlayer spacing shows that the injection of oxygen-containing groups hinders the development of the material structure from disorder to order, indicating the slightly enhanced cross-linking strength in WFOC250-1400 and WFOC280-1400.</p>
         
         <p>XRD pattern was employed to explore the microstructure of these three samples obtained under various preparation processes. From Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> (Supporting Information), two typical diffraction broad peaks ≈ 22–25° (002) and 42–45° (100) were observed, indicating that they are all typical highly disordered hard carbon material with graphite microcrystals. Additionally, previous research<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0037_R_d47241363e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Nano">37</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0038_R_d47241363e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> has revealed that the sodium storage performance of hard carbon materials can be reflected in the degree of graphitization. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307132-fig-0002">2a–c</a>, peak fitting on the expanded (002) diffraction peak has been carried out with the aim of clearly determining the distinct regional distribution in various samples. The area of disordered regions in waste-derived carbon materials gradually increases to 65% from 52%, whereas the corresponding graphitized regions begin to shrink as oxygen concentration rises. The growth of hard carbon materials is encouraged by an abundance of oxygen-containing flaws, which also lengthen the space between graphene sheets to allow for more Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation. This outcome supports the reasonableness of the fitted data and is very compatible with the HRTEM analysis.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307132-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23c05a6f-b5d1-4ded-be93-6a54c79964ce/smll202307132-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/23c05a6f-b5d1-4ded-be93-6a54c79964ce/smll202307132-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b9738633-8953-41b2-92ad-19a64493448d/smll202307132-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23c05a6f-b5d1-4ded-be93-6a54c79964ce/smll202307132-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307132-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) Fitted XRD spectra, and d–f) fitted Raman spectra of WFC1400, WFOC250-1400, and WFOC280-1400, respectively. g) The peak area ratio of D band and G band in fitted Raman spectra and XRD pattern. h) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and i) BJH pore-size distribution curves of corresponding samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further identify the structural defects and disorder degree of three samples, the microstructure was also analyzed using Raman spectroscopy. From Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a> (Supporting Information), it can be observed that the fitted Raman spectrum has two different characteristic bands at ≈ 1350&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (D-band) and 1580&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (G-band), indicating that the obtained samples are all hard carbon-based materials. Then in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0002">2d–f</a>, D-band can be further subdivided into D, D<sub>2</sub> (1610&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), D<sub>3</sub> (1490&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), and D<sub>4</sub> (1200&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0039_R_d47241363e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0040_R_d47241363e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon N Y">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity ratio of D-band (I<sub>D</sub>) to G-band (I<sub>G</sub>) is usually utilized to investigate the disorder degree in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0041_R_d47241363e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0002">2d–f</a>, the value of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of WFC1400, WFOC250-1400, and WFOC280-1400 are increase from 1.175, 1.18, and 1.198, respectively, proves that pre-oxidation methods promote the disordered properties of WFOC, which is consistent with HRTEM characterization. On the side, D<sub>3</sub>-band originates from the vibration of short-range sp<sup>3</sup> carbon in amorphous carbon, therefore, the peak area ratio A<sub>D3</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> can indicate the amount of amorphous carbon and functional groups containing oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0042_R_d47241363e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon N Y">42</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0043_R_d47241363e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the A<sub>D3</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> value achieves its highest point during WFOC280-1400, showing that there is an increase in the amount of amorphous graphene. Based on these spectral analyses, it can be confirmed that the WFOC-T material has more disordered regions, which is consistent with the XRD spectrum mentioned above.</p>
         
         <p>And Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0002">2h</a> shows the nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherm and pore size distribution of waste-derived carbon, and the information of BET surface area is presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The type IV isotherm with an adsorption hysteresis loop is present in all three samples, demonstrating the existence of mesopores and microporous structures in waste-derived carbon. The hysteresis loop that appears in the high relative pressure zone further validates the presence of mesopores of various volumes and sizes in the material, while the abrupt increase of the curve in the low relative pressure region reveals the presence of micropores. Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the specific surface areas of WFC1400, WFOC250-1400, and WFOC280-1400 decrease with increasing oxygen defect content, with values of 52.1, 15.3, and 10.8&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It can be seen that the larger specific surface area of WFC1400 samples obtained by direct carbonization is mainly due to the presence of mesopores. In WFOC-T samples, a certain amount of oxygen-containing groups can promote dehydration and strong cross-linking of polymer chains, and the internal graphite microcrystal growth and bending to generate closed pores. And the continuous decrease of specific surface area in WFOC280-1400 can be attributed to the excessive etching of oxygen-containing groups on the carbon layer surface. According to BJH pore size analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0002">2i</a>), WFOC250-1400 samples with a smaller pore volume have a concentrated pore size distribution at 2–4&nbsp;nm. The size of WFOC280-1400 continues to increase with the increase of oxidation temperature, and internal overpressure and smaller pores aggregate in the material, resulting in the increase of pores size. Thus, the moderate oxygen defect content can promote the regulation of the pore structure of carbon materials, which is conducive to the rapid transport and storage of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, XPS analysis shows that this pre-oxidation technology has successfully introduced oxygen-containing functional groups into WFOC-T materials. In the full spectrum of XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a>, Supporting Information), it is known that all three samples are composed of C and O elements. As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), while the heat temperature increases, the proportion of O content continues to increase from 3.66 at% to 5.94 at%, indicating that oxygen-containing groups have been effectively mixed into the WFOC-T material. The higher oxygen content provides a more active site for Na<sup>+</sup> in the turbine layer nanocrystals,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0044_R_d47241363e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is also the reason why WFOC-T materials have higher specific capacity. In addition, for the C1s fitting spectrum, the four main peaks located at 284.2, 284.8, 285.5, 286.4, and 290.3&nbsp;eV correspond to sp<sup>2</sup>-C, sp<sup>3</sup>-C, C─O, C═O, and ─COOR carboxyl groups, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307132-fig-0003">3a–c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0045_R_d47241363e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">45</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0046_R_d47241363e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Sustain. Energy Fuels">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) also shows that the peak area ratio of sp<sup>2</sup>-C exists in defect-free graphite lattices declines, while the C─O and C═O bonds present in defect graphite lattices increase, further proving the increase in disorder of WFOC-T materials. Meanwhile, in the O 1s fitting feature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0003">3d–f</a>), as the content of oxygen-containing groups increases, the intensity of the C─O bonds and ─COOR centered at 533.8 and 535.8&nbsp;eV decreases, while the C═O group centered at 532.7&nbsp;eV steadily increases, which is consistent with the above trend.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0047_R_d47241363e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Chem. A">47</a>, <a href="#smll202307132-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0048_R_d47241363e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Solid State Ionics">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To be specific, the ratio of C═O increased to ≈63% from ≈44% while the C═O was proved to helps the reversible adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> by carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0049_R_d47241363e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, XPS spectral analysis provides strong evidence that the pre-oxidation method introduces more oxygen-containing groups that will provide more efficient active site and wield significant impact on the physical and chemical properties of WFOC-T materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307132-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/41cc19db-a300-42d9-9dd9-5a1887606be7/smll202307132-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/41cc19db-a300-42d9-9dd9-5a1887606be7/smll202307132-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b266450e-6d8a-4452-9b55-f066cfdbd99c/smll202307132-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/41cc19db-a300-42d9-9dd9-5a1887606be7/smll202307132-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307132-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Fitted XPS survey scan and high resolution C 1s a–c) and O 1s d–f) spectra of the WFC1400, WFCO250-1400 and WFOC280-1400, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the sodium storage performance of waste-derived carbon, all samples were assembled into 2032 coin-type batteries. Among them, Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is carried out at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 0–2&nbsp;V. As seen in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307132-fig-0004">4a–c</a>, the cross-sectional photographs of the samples exhibit alike shapes, demonstrating that the sodium storage mechanism is similar.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0050_R_d47241363e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nano Energy">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the appearance of a pair of sharp oxidation/reduction peaks at 0.01–0.1&nbsp;V is mainly due to the extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in graphene layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0051_R_d47241363e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nano Lett.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, the appearance of two irreversible reduction peaks of ≈ 0.47 and 0.93&nbsp;V can be attributed to the partial decomposition of the electrolyte on the electrode surface, the formation of the solid electrolyte interface layer (SEI), and the side reactions between the electrolyte and surface functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0052_R_d47241363e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formation of a stable SEI layer causes irreversible decay of initial capacity. In WFOC250-1400, the irreversible peak intensity at almost 0.47&nbsp;V decreased, which can be explained by a higher C═O bond content/ratio and smaller specific surface area, which promotes its higher ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0049_R_d47241363e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in WFOC280-1400 with content of oxygen functional groups as high as 5.94 at%, the irreversible peak strength near 0.47&nbsp;V once again increases, which also means that the excessive C─O bond influence the formation of the SEI layer and generate greater irreversible capacity. Moreover, pre-oxidation with higher temperatures could etch the carbon and form large pores, causing the reduction of ICE. The CV curve scan contour obtained in the subsequent cycle has good overlap, indicating that a stable SEI layer has been formed in the first cycle, and the sample has good reversibility and electrochemical stability during the charge/discharge process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307132-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/836b1f9e-625c-4c2d-9872-50f731fcc783/smll202307132-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/836b1f9e-625c-4c2d-9872-50f731fcc783/smll202307132-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/56140345-f410-4086-8522-1b0943eee334/smll202307132-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/836b1f9e-625c-4c2d-9872-50f731fcc783/smll202307132-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307132-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) CV curves of three samples, and d) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves and e) cycling stability of WFC1400, WFOC250-1400, and WFOC280-1400. f) Charge and discharge profiles and g) cycling performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/WFOC250-1400 full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>And the initial galvanostatic charge/discharge capacity, first cycle Coulombic efficiency (ICE), and the proportion of slope/plateau regions in the total capacity of different WFC-T and WFOC-T samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0004">4d</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). According to Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), it can be seen that the oxygen content of 5.41 at% in WFOC250-1400 can be used for highly reversible sodium storage at a charging capacity of 308.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with 90.1% ICE while the oxygen content in WFOC280-1400 material increases to 5.94 at%, the capacity actually decreases to ≈290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The significant capacity difference between the two samples can be attributed to two aspects: 1) the introduction of oxygen regulates the degree of graphitization of WFOC-T, resulting in the interlayer spacing of the carbon layer increased while WFOC250-1400 achieving the suitable one ≈ 0.382&nbsp;nm. 2) the critical content of oxygen-containing groups, below or above that the insertion and removal of sodium ions will become more difficult. Furthermore, A certain quantity of oxygen can be essential to improving the wettability of the materials with the electrolyte. But a larger oxygen concentration during the initial charging and discharging cycle may cause electrolyte breakdown and the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI), boosting irreversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0053_R_d47241363e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In detail, the key to initial capacity loss (low ICE) lies in the decomposition of electrolytes in the electrode material, the formation of SEI layers on the surface of carbon materials, and a series of side reactions between electrolytes and structural defects. The ICE of directly carbonized WFC1400 material is 81.1% while the WFOC-T sample exhibits higher reversible capacity and ICE, which may be due to the smaller specific surface area, which reduces the contact area between the electrode and electrolyte, as well as an increase in the proportion of disordered regions, providing a larger interlayer distance for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. In addition, although the different proportions of slope and platform areas may vary depending on different distinguishing conditions (0.1 vs 0.13&nbsp;V), the WFOC250-1400 sample under the pre-oxidation process has the optimal slope and plateau proportion, indicating a good charge transfer process and sodium ion diffusion performance (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). In all samples, WFOC250-1400 provides high electrochemical performance due to its concentrated micropores and appropriate defect content, providing a more active site for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. As the oxygen content continues to increase, the reversible capacity and ICE of WFOC280-1400 samples begin to decrease. This indicates that there exists a balance of structure with a degree of graphitization, pore, and oxygen-containing groups to achieve a great sodium storage performance, which is closely related to the results observed in the characterization analysis. Besides, the control of the carbonization temperature is also crucial for the effectiveness of electrochemical performance. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), as the carbonization temperature increases, the plateau capacity gradually increases, which is attributed to the higher degree of graphitization and decreased interlayer distance, indicating the important role of carbonization temperature. Then the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements of three samples that utilized to further explore how conductivity and electrochemical performance are affected by oxygen-containing group content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information). In the Nyquist plot, the semicircle in the middle and high-frequency region is related to SEI resistance and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>), while the straight line in the low-frequency region is mainly related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion resistance (W). The smaller semicircle and steeper slope of the straight line indicate that the WFOC-1400 electrode has lower charge transfer resistance and ion diffusion resistance, indicating a good charge transfer process and sodium ion diffusion performance.</p>
         
         <p>As for the cycling stability of the three electrodes, the constant current charge/discharge cycling test at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0004">4e</a>. Although they have different specific capacities and ICE, all WFC-T and WFOC-T electrodes exhibit good cycling performance due to the inherent structural stability of amorphous carbon. The WFOC250-1400 electrode can maintain a capacity retention of ≈85% after 100 cycles, which is much higher than the WFC1400 electrode. This variation in cycle stability can be related to moderate disordered structures, which encourage effective interaction between the electrolyte and carbon atoms, and offer more transport pathways for the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the practical application prospects of WFOC250-1400 electrodes, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was selected as the cathode side and assembled into a full coin-type battery to test the structural stability. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0004">4f</a>, the full cell is operated in NaPF<sub>6</sub>+EC/DMC+4% FEC electrolyte with a voltage range of 0–3.8&nbsp;V. The activated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/WFOC250-1400 full cell provides a reversible capacity of 237.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 79.95% ICE at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;C, with an average output voltage of 3.2&nbsp;V. The energy density of this full cell was calculated ≈ 205.9&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total active-mass about cathode and anode. The high energy density and low-cost property indicate the practical application potential of WFOC materials. After 100 cycles, the WFOC250-1400 electrode still has a 50% capacity retention rate, indicating that it has certain advantages as a sodium storage material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0004">4g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>In addition, to analyze the diffusion kinetics of WFC-T and WFOC-T electrode materials in detail, the constant current intermittent titration (GITT) curve is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307132-fig-0005">5a–d</a>. It can be observed that the variation trend of ion diffusion coefficients in WFC1400 and WFOC250-1400 materials is basically similar during both the charge and discharge process. And the almost opposite change in apparent diffusion coefficient during the charging process indicates that the charging and discharging process is highly reversible. Since the selected data is the first cycle of GITT data, the diffusion coefficient shows a decreasing trend in the range of ≈ 0.8 to 0.6&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0005">5c</a>), mainly contributed to the formation of a stable SEI layer by the electrode material during the first discharge process, which hinders the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0007_R_d47241363e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Throughout the entire electrochemical process, the continuous decrease of D<sub>Na+</sub> from the slope region to the plateau region indicates that the intercalation process of Na<sup>+</sup> within the carbon layer is more difficult to occur than the surface adsorption process. This is mainly due to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion needs to overcome the repulsive force between the defect site and the original Na<sup>+</sup>. A sufficient number of oxygen-containing functional groups encourages the WFOC250-1400 electrode to have a higher D<sub>Na+</sub> in slope area in comparison to the WFC1400 electrode. When distinguishing the slope and plateau regions based on the specific voltage distribution of GITT ion diffusion (≈0.13&nbsp;V) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), it is not difficult to find that, compared to non-oxidized samples, the WFOC250-1400 material as an electrode material exhibits better adsorption capacity and embedding capacity. Before the limit voltage, the diffusion coefficient slightly increased, indicating in addition to Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and embedding, it may also include the deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> in closed pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307132-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307132-bib-0054_R_d47241363e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the storage mechanism of sodium ions in waste-derived carbon electrodes can be roughly divided into three parts (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307132-fig-0005">5e</a>): 1) the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface of the carbon layer and defect sites in the high voltage slope region; 2) The intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> in the low voltage plateau region between the turbine layer graphene sheets; 3) The filling of Na<sup>+</sup> in the enclosed voids of the turbine layer in the region close to 0.01&nbsp;V.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307132-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c9f78e39-7e1b-4df8-aac8-16b662a6c5cf/smll202307132-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c9f78e39-7e1b-4df8-aac8-16b662a6c5cf/smll202307132-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/42fc0be5-0cb9-400e-819e-fe428286606c/smll202307132-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c9f78e39-7e1b-4df8-aac8-16b662a6c5cf/smll202307132-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307132-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) GITT curves, and c–d) corresponding D<sub>Na+</sub> of WFC1400 and WFOC250-1400. e) Schematic diagram of sodium storage mechanism of WFOC250-1400.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307132-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307132-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Waste foam can be effectively transformed through the pre-oxidation process into meaningful carbon-based energy storage materials, which is in keeping with the concept of sustainable economic development. Thus, by regulating the amount of oxygen-containing groups and disorder degree, we have successfully manufactured a high-performance SIB anode from waste foam utilizing pre-oxidation technology. The optimized waste-derived carbon possess great sodium storage performance, exhibiting a reversible specific capacity of 308.0 at 20&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with an ICE of 90.1%, and the capacity retention rate is 85% after 100 cycles. And the assembled full cell measurements also showed promising reversible capacity, providing feasibility for practical high-energy density SIBs. Furthermore, the sodium storage mechanism of waste-derived carbon materials is adsorption, embedding, and pore filling, which is observed by GITT analysis data. More importantly, this pre-oxidation strategy is efficient and practical, and can also be applied to improve carbon yield while obtaining high sodium storage performance, providing a new idea for the development of advanced low-cost SIBs anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
